<p><b>Pelicans</b> are a <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> of large <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> that makes up the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <b>Pelecanidae</b>. They are characterised by a long <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">beak</a> and a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Throat_pouch" title="Throat pouch">throat pouch</a> used for catching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prey" title="Prey">prey</a> and draining water from the scooped up contents before swallowing. They have predominantly pale plumage, the exceptions being the <a href="/wiki/Brown_pelican" title="Brown pelican">brown</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_pelican" title="Peruvian pelican">Peruvian pelicans</a>. The bills, pouches and bare facial skin of all species become brightly coloured before the breeding season. The eight living pelican species have a patchy global distribution, ranging <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudinally</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_zone" title="Temperate zone">temperate zone</a>, though they are absent from interior South America as well as from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar regions</a> and the open ocean.</p>
<p>Long thought to be related to <a href="/wiki/Frigatebird" title="Frigatebird">frigatebirds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tropicbird" title="Tropicbird">tropicbirds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulidae" title="Sulidae">gannets and boobies</a>, pelicans instead are now known to be most closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Shoebill" title="Shoebill">shoebill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamerkop" title="Hamerkop">hamerkop</a>, and are placed in the order <a href="/wiki/Pelecaniformes" title="Pelecaniformes">Pelecaniformes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ibis" title="Ibis">Ibises</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spoonbill" title="Spoonbill">spoonbills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a> and the desolate <a href="/wiki/Bittern" title="Bittern">bitterns</a> have been classified in the same order. Fossil evidence of pelicans dates back to at least 30 million years to the remains of a beak very similar to that of modern species recovered from <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> strata in France. They are thought to have evolved in the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> and spread into the Americas; this is reflected in the relationships within the genus as the eight species divide into Old World and <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> lineages.</p>
<p>Pelicans frequent inland and coastal waters where they feed principally on fish, catching them at or near the water surface. They are <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gregarious" title="wikt:gregarious">gregarious</a> birds, travelling in flocks, <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_hunting" title="Cooperative hunting">hunting cooperatively</a> and breeding <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">colonially</a>. Four white-plumaged species tend to nest on the ground, and four brown or grey-plumaged species nest mainly in trees. The relationship between pelicans and people has often been contentious. The birds have been persecuted because of their perceived competition with commercial and recreational fishing. Their populations have fallen through habitat destruction, disturbance and environmental pollution, and three species are of conservation concern. They also have a long history of cultural significance in <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a>, and in Christian and <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldic</a> <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_systematics">Taxonomy and systematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and systematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The genus <i>Pelecanus</i> was first formally described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> in 1758 in the <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">tenth edition</a> of his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>. He described the distinguishing characteristics as a straight bill hooked at the tip, linear nostrils, a bare face, and fully webbed feet. This early definition included <a href="/wiki/Frigatebird" title="Frigatebird">frigatebirds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorants</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulidae" title="Sulidae">sulids</a> as well as pelicans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-linnaeus_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-linnaeus-1">[1]</a></sup> The name comes from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> word <i>pelekan</i> (πελεκάν),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> which is itself derived from the word <i>pelekys</i> (πέλεκυς) meaning "axe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">classical</a> times, the word was applied to both the pelican and the woodpecker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pelecaniformes#Systematics_and_evolution" title="Pelecaniformes">Pelecaniformes § Systematics and evolution</a></div>
<p>The family Pelecanidae was introduced (as Pelicanea) by the French <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_Samuel_Rafinesque" title="Constantine Samuel Rafinesque">Constantine Samuel Rafinesque</a> in 1815.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Pelicans give their name to the <a href="/wiki/Pelecaniformes" title="Pelecaniformes">Pelecaniformes</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> which has a varied taxonomic history. <a href="/wiki/Tropicbird" title="Tropicbird">Tropicbirds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Darter" title="Darter">darters</a>, cormorants, <a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">gannets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Booby" title="Booby">boobies</a> and frigatebirds, all traditional members of the order, have since been reclassified: tropicbirds into their own order, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phaethontiformes" title="Phaethontiformes">Phaethontiformes</a>, and the remainder into <a href="/wiki/Suliformes" title="Suliformes">Suliformes</a>. In their place, <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibis" title="Ibis">ibises</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spoonbill" title="Spoonbill">spoonbills</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hamerkop" title="Hamerkop">hamerkop</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shoebill" title="Shoebill">shoebill</a> have now been transferred into Pelecaniformes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hackett_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hackett-7">[7]</a></sup> Molecular evidence suggests that the shoebill and the hamerkop form a <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a> to the pelicans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith2010_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith2010-8">[8]</a></sup> though there is some doubt as to the exact relationship between the three lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_record">Fossil record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fossil record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The fossil record shows that the pelican lineage has existed for at least 30 million years; the oldest known pelican fossil was found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Oligocene" title="Early Oligocene">Early Oligocene</a> deposits at the <a href="/wiki/Luberon" title="Luberon">Luberon</a> in southeastern France and is remarkably similar to modern forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louchart2010_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Louchart2010-10">[10]</a></sup> Its beak is almost complete and is morphologically identical to that of present-day pelicans, showing that this advanced feeding apparatus was already in existence at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louchart2010_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Louchart2010-10">[10]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Early_Miocene" title="Early Miocene">Early Miocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> has been named <i><a href="/wiki/Miopelecanus" title="Miopelecanus">Miopelecanus gracilis</a></i> on the basis of certain features originally considered unique but later thought to lie within the range of inter-specific variation in <i>Pelecanus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louchart2010_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Louchart2010-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Eocene" title="Late Eocene">Late Eocene</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Protopelicanus" title="Protopelicanus">Protopelicanus</a></i> may be a pelecaniform or suliform – or a similar aquatic bird such as a pseudotooth (<a href="/wiki/Pelagornithidae" title="Pelagornithidae">Pelagornithidae</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The supposed <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> pelican <i><a href="/wiki/Liptornis" title="Liptornis">Liptornis</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> is a <i><a href="/wiki/Nomen_dubium" title="Nomen dubium">nomen dubium</a></i> (of doubtful validity), being based on fragments providing insufficient evidence to support a valid description.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fossil finds from North America have been meagre compared with Europe, which has a richer fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbsw_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbsw-13">[13]</a></sup> Several <i>Pelecanus</i> species have been described from fossil material, including:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fbbm_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-fbbm-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pelecanus_cadimurka" title="Pelecanus cadimurka">Pelecanus cadimurka</a></i>, Rich &amp; van Tets, 1981 (Late Pliocene, South Australia)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pelecanus_cautleyi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pelecanus cautleyi (page does not exist)">Pelecanus cautleyi</a></i>, Davies, 1880 (Early Pliocene, Siwalik Hills, India)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fbbm_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-fbbm-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pelecanus_fraasi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pelecanus fraasi (page does not exist)">Pelecanus fraasi</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lydekker" title="Richard Lydekker">Lydekker</a>, 1891 (Middle Miocene, Bavaria, Germany)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fbbm_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-fbbm-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelecanus_gracilis" title="Pelecanus gracilis">Pelecanus gracilis</a></i>, Milne-Edwards, 1863 (Early Miocene, France) (see: <i><a href="/wiki/Miopelecanus" title="Miopelecanus">Miopelecanus</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fbbm_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-fbbm-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pelecanus_halieus" title="Pelecanus halieus">Pelecanus halieus</a></i>, Wetmore, 1933 (Late Pliocene, Idaho, US)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Pelecanus intermedius</i>, Fraas, 1870 (Middle Miocene, Bavaria, Germany)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fbbm_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-fbbm-14">[14]</a></sup> (transferred to <i>Miopelecanus</i> by Cheneval in 1984)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pelecanus_odessanus" title="Pelecanus odessanus">Pelecanus odessanus</a></i>, Widhalm, 1886 (Late Miocene, near Odessa, Ukraine)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pelecanus_schreiberi" title="Pelecanus schreiberi">Pelecanus schreiberi</a></i>, Olson, 1999 (Early Pliocene, North Carolina, US)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbsw_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-pbsw-13">[13]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pelecanus_sivalensis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pelecanus sivalensis (page does not exist)">Pelecanus sivalensis</a></i>, Davies, 1880 (Early Pliocene, Siwalik Hills, India)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fbbm_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-fbbm-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pelecanus_tirarensis" title="Pelecanus tirarensis">Pelecanus tirarensis</a></i>, Miller, 1966 (Late Oligocene to Middle Miocene, South Australia)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Living_species">Living species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Living species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The eight living pelican species were traditionally divided into two groups, one containing four ground-nesters with mainly white adult plumage (<a href="/wiki/Australian_pelican" title="Australian pelican">Australian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dalmatian_pelican" title="Dalmatian pelican">Dalmatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_white_pelican" title="Great white pelican">great white</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_white_pelican" title="American white pelican">American white pelicans</a>), and one containing four grey or brown plumaged species which nest preferentially either in trees (<a href="/wiki/Pink-backed_pelican" title="Pink-backed pelican">pink-backed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spot-billed_pelican" title="Spot-billed pelican">spot-billed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brown_pelican" title="Brown pelican">brown pelicans</a>), or on sea rocks (<a href="/wiki/Peruvian_pelican" title="Peruvian pelican">Peruvian pelican</a>). The largely marine brown and Peruvian pelicans, formerly considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspecific" title="Conspecific">conspecific</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup> are sometimes separated from the others by placement in the <a href="/wiki/Subgenus" title="Subgenus">subgenus</a> <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Leptopelicanus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Leptopelicanus (page does not exist)">Leptopelicanus</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> but in fact species with both sorts of appearance and nesting behavior are found in either.</p>
<p>DNA sequencing of both mitochondrial and nuclear genes yielded relationships quite different; the three <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> pelicans formed one lineage, with the American white pelican sister to the two brown pelicans, and the five <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> species the other. The Dalmatian, pink-backed and spot-billed were all closely related to one another, while the Australian white pelican was their next-closest relative. The great white pelican also belonged to this lineage but was the first to diverge from the common ancestor of the other four species. This finding suggests that pelicans evolved in the Old World and spread into the Americas, and that preference for tree- or ground-nesting is more related to size than genetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>

<td><a href="/wiki/American_white_pelican" title="American white pelican">American white pelican</a><br/>
<i>Pelecanus erythrorhynchos</i><br/>
Gmelin, 1789</td>
<td>
<center><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mikebaird_-_American_White_Pelican_(Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_)_(bird)_in_Mo_(by).jpg"><img alt="American white pelican" data-file-height="1686" data-file-width="2115" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mikebaird_-_American_White_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_%29_%28bird%29_in_Mo_%28by%29.jpg/185px-Mikebaird_-_American_White_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_%29_%28bird%29_in_Mo_%28by%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mikebaird_-_American_White_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_%29_%28bird%29_in_Mo_%28by%29.jpg/278px-Mikebaird_-_American_White_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_%29_%28bird%29_in_Mo_%28by%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mikebaird_-_American_White_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_%29_%28bird%29_in_Mo_%28by%29.jpg/370px-Mikebaird_-_American_White_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_%29_%28bird%29_in_Mo_%28by%29.jpg 2x" width="185"/></a></center>
</td>
<td>Length 1.3–1.8 m (4.3–5.9 ft), wingspan 2.44–2.9 m (8.0–9.5 ft), weight 5–9 kg (10–20 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nellis01_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nellis01-23">[23]</a></sup> Plumage almost entirely white, except for black primary and secondary <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Remiges" title="Flight feather">remiges</a> only visible in flight.</td>
<td>Monotypic. Inland North America, wintering in Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sibleymonroe_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-sibleymonroe-24">[24]</a></sup> Status: Least Concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucnerythrorhynchos_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-iucnerythrorhynchos-25">[25]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Brown_pelican" title="Brown pelican">Brown pelican</a><br/>
<i>Pelecanus occidentalis</i><br/>
Linnaeus, 1766</td>
<td>
<center><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelecanus_Occidentalis_KW_1.JPG"><img alt="Brown pelican" data-file-height="2316" data-file-width="3396" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Pelecanus_Occidentalis_KW_1.JPG/185px-Pelecanus_Occidentalis_KW_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Pelecanus_Occidentalis_KW_1.JPG/278px-Pelecanus_Occidentalis_KW_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Pelecanus_Occidentalis_KW_1.JPG/370px-Pelecanus_Occidentalis_KW_1.JPG 2x" width="185"/></a></center>
</td>
<td>Length up to 1.4 m (4.6 ft), wingspan 2–2.3 m (6.6–7.5 ft), weight 3.6–4.5 kg (7.9–9.9 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Smallest pelican; distinguished by brown plumage; feeds by plunge-diving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></td>
<td>Five subspecies. Coastal distribution ranging from North America and the Caribbean to northern South America and the Galapagos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sibleymonroe_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-sibleymonroe-24">[24]</a></sup> Status: Least Concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucnoccidentalis_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-iucnoccidentalis-28">[28]</a></sup></td>
</tr>

<center><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pel%C3%ADcano_en_Pucusana.JPG"><img alt="Peruvian pelican" data-file-height="1165" data-file-width="1553" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Pel%C3%ADcano_en_Pucusana.JPG/185px-Pel%C3%ADcano_en_Pucusana.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Pel%C3%ADcano_en_Pucusana.JPG/278px-Pel%C3%ADcano_en_Pucusana.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Pel%C3%ADcano_en_Pucusana.JPG/370px-Pel%C3%ADcano_en_Pucusana.JPG 2x" width="185"/></a></center>
</td>
<td>Length up to 1.52 m (5.0 ft), wingspan 2.48 m (8.1 ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> average weight 7 kg (15 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Dark with a white stripe from the crown down the sides of the neck.</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Monotypic_taxon" title="Monotypic taxon">Monotypic</a>. Pacific Coast of South America from Ecuador and Peru south through to southern Chile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sibleymonroe_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-sibleymonroe-24">[24]</a></sup> Status: Near Threatened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucnthagus_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-iucnthagus-31">[31]</a></sup></td>

<td>
<center><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whitepelican_edit_shadowlift_edit.jpg"><img alt="Great white pelican" data-file-height="2088" data-file-width="3042" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Whitepelican_edit_shadowlift_edit.jpg/185px-Whitepelican_edit_shadowlift_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Whitepelican_edit_shadowlift_edit.jpg/278px-Whitepelican_edit_shadowlift_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Whitepelican_edit_shadowlift_edit.jpg/370px-Whitepelican_edit_shadowlift_edit.jpg 2x" width="185"/></a></center>
</td>
<td>Length 1.40–1.75 m (4.6–5.7 ft), wingspan 2.45–2.95 m (8.0–9.7 ft), weight 10–11 kg (22–24 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BWP_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mullarney_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-33">[33]</a></sup> Plumage white, with pink facial patch and legs.</td>
<td>Monotypic. Patchy distribution from eastern Mediterranean east to Indochina and Malay Peninsula, and south to South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sibleymonroe_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-sibleymonroe-24">[24]</a></sup> Status: Least Concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucnonocrot_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-iucnonocrot-34">[34]</a></sup></td>
</tr>

<td>Length 1.60–1.90 m (5.2–6.2 ft), wingspan 2.5–3.4 m (8.2–11.2 ft), weight 4–8.2 kg (8.8–18.1 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Predominantly white with black along primaries and very large, pale pink bill.</td>
<td>Monotypic. Australia and New Guinea; vagrant to New Zealand, Solomons, Bismarck Archipelago, Fiji and <a href="/wiki/Wallacea" title="Wallacea">Wallacea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sibleymonroe_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-sibleymonroe-24">[24]</a></sup> Status: Least Concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucnconspic_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-iucnconspic-36">[36]</a></sup></td>
</tr>

<td>Monotypic. Africa, Seychelles and southwestern Arabia;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sibleymonroe_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-sibleymonroe-24">[24]</a></sup> extinct in Madagascar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Status: Least Concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucnrufescens_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-iucnrufescens-40">[40]</a></sup></td>

</td>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelican-Gaping-Aruba.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="2128" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Pelican-Gaping-Aruba.JPG/220px-Pelican-Gaping-Aruba.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Pelican-Gaping-Aruba.JPG/330px-Pelican-Gaping-Aruba.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Pelican-Gaping-Aruba.JPG/440px-Pelican-Gaping-Aruba.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_-Tulsa_Zoo,_Oklahoma,_USA-8c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="816" data-file-width="671" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_-Tulsa_Zoo%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-8c.jpg/170px-Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_-Tulsa_Zoo%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-8c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_-Tulsa_Zoo%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-8c.jpg/255px-Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_-Tulsa_Zoo%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-8c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_-Tulsa_Zoo%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-8c.jpg/340px-Pelecanus_erythrorhynchos_-Tulsa_Zoo%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-8c.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>

<a href="/wiki/American_white_pelican" title="American white pelican">American white pelican</a> with knob which develops on bill before the breeding season</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Smith_Island,_Chesapeake_Bay,_Maryland,_USA_-nest-8cr.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2021" data-file-width="2639" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Smith_Island%2C_Chesapeake_Bay%2C_Maryland%2C_USA_-nest-8cr.jpg/220px-Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Smith_Island%2C_Chesapeake_Bay%2C_Maryland%2C_USA_-nest-8cr.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Smith_Island%2C_Chesapeake_Bay%2C_Maryland%2C_USA_-nest-8cr.jpg/330px-Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Smith_Island%2C_Chesapeake_Bay%2C_Maryland%2C_USA_-nest-8cr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Smith_Island%2C_Chesapeake_Bay%2C_Maryland%2C_USA_-nest-8cr.jpg/440px-Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Smith_Island%2C_Chesapeake_Bay%2C_Maryland%2C_USA_-nest-8cr.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Smith_Island,_Chesapeake_Bay,_Maryland,_USA_-nest-8cr.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An adult <a href="/wiki/Brown_pelican" title="Brown pelican">brown pelican</a> with a chick in a nest in Chesapeake Bay, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, US. This species will nest on the ground when no suitable trees are available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pelicans are very large birds with very long bills characterised by a downcurved hook at the end of the upper mandible, and the attachment of a huge <a href="/wiki/Gular_skin" title="Gular skin">gular</a> pouch to the lower. The slender <a href="/wiki/Ramus_of_the_mandible" title="Ramus of the mandible">rami</a> of the lower bill and the flexible tongue muscles form the pouch into a basket for catching fish and, sometimes, rainwater,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> though in order not to hinder the swallowing of large fish, the tongue itself is tiny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> They have a long neck and short stout legs with large, fully webbed feet. Although they are among the heaviest of flying birds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> they are relatively light for their apparent bulk because of air pockets in the skeleton and beneath the skin enabling them to float high in the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> The tail is short and square. The wings are long and broad, suitably shaped for soaring and gliding flight, and have the unusually large number of 30 to 35 secondary <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">flight feathers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Males are generally larger than females and have longer bills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> The smallest species is the brown pelican, small individuals of which can be no more than 2.75 kg (6.1 lb) and 1.06 m (3.5 ft) long, with a wingspan of as little as 1.83 m (6.0 ft). The largest is believed to be the Dalmatian, at up to 15 kg (33 lb) and 1.83 m (6.0 ft) in length, with a maximum wingspan of 3 m (9.8 ft). The Australian pelican's bill may grow up to 0.5 m (1.6 ft) long in large males,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1c_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1c-48">[48]</a></sup> the longest of any bird.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pelicans have mainly light-coloured plumage, the exceptions being the brown and Peruvian pelicans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mb_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-mb-49">[49]</a></sup> The bills, pouches and bare facial skin of all species become brighter before breeding season commences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-enc-50">[50]</a></sup> The throat pouch of the Californian subspecies of the brown pelican turns bright red, and fades to yellow after the eggs are laid, while the throat pouch of the Peruvian pelican turns blue. The American white pelican grows a prominent knob on its bill that is shed once females have laid eggs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith05_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keith05-51">[51]</a></sup> The plumage of immature pelicans is darker than that of adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mb_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-mb-49">[49]</a></sup> Newly hatched chicks are naked and pink, darkening to grey or black after 4 to 14 days, then developing a covering of white or grey <a href="/wiki/Down_feather" title="Down feather">down</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DofB_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-DofB-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_sacs">Air sacs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Air sacs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Anatomical dissections of two brown pelicans in 1939 showed that pelicans have a network of subcutaneous <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy#Skeletal_system" title="Bird anatomy">air sacs</a> under their skin situated across the ventral surface including the throat, breast and undersides of the wings, as well as having air sacs in their bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richardson39_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-richardson39-53">[53]</a></sup> The air sacs are connected to the airways of the respiratory system, and the pelican can keep its air sacs inflated by closing its <a href="/wiki/Glottis" title="Glottis">glottis</a>, but it is not clear how air sacs are inflated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richardson39_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-richardson39-53">[53]</a></sup> The air sacs serve to keep the pelican remarkably buoyant in the water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and may also cushion the impact of the pelican's body on the water surface when they dive from flight into water to catch fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richardson39_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-richardson39-53">[53]</a></sup> Superficial air sacs may also help to round body contours (especially over the abdomen where surface protuberances may be caused by viscera changing size and position) to enable the overlying feathers to form more effective heat insulation and also to enable feathers to be held in position for good aerodynamics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richardson39_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-richardson39-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Modern pelicans are found on all continents except Antarctica. They primarily inhabit warm regions, although breeding ranges extend to latitudes of 45° South (Australian pelicans in <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>) and 60° North (American white pelicans in western Canada).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup> Birds of inland and coastal waters, they are absent from polar regions, the deep ocean, oceanic islands (except the Galapagos), and inland South America, as well as from the eastern coast of South America from the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a> southwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> Subfossil bones have been recovered from as far south as New Zealand's <a href="/wiki/South_Island" title="South Island">South Island</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gill91_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-gill91-55">[55]</a></sup> although their scarcity and isolated occurrence suggests that these remains may have merely been vagrants from Australia (much as is the case today).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour_and_ecology">Behaviour and ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Behaviour and ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Australian_pelican_in_flight.jpg"><img alt="Australian pelican gliding" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1067" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Australian_pelican_in_flight.jpg/170px-Australian_pelican_in_flight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Australian_pelican_in_flight.jpg/255px-Australian_pelican_in_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Australian_pelican_in_flight.jpg/340px-Australian_pelican_in_flight.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Australian_pelican_in_flight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An Australian pelican gliding with its large wings extended</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pelicans swim well with their strong legs and their webbed feet. They rub the backs of their heads on their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preen_gland" title="Preen gland">preen glands</a> to pick up an oily secretion, which they transfer to their <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> to waterproof it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup> Holding their wings only loosely against their bodies, pelicans float with relatively little of their bodies below the water surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beaman_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-beaman-37">[37]</a></sup> They dissipate excess heat by gular flutter – rippling the skin of the throat and pouch with the bill open to promote <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaporative_cooling" title="Evaporative cooling">evaporative cooling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> They roost and loaf communally on beaches, sandbanks and in shallow water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-6"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>A fibrous layer deep in the breast muscles can hold the wings rigidly horizontal for gliding and soaring. Thus they use <a href="/wiki/Thermal" title="Thermal">thermals</a> for soaring to heights of 3000 m (10,000 ft) or more,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-ag-57">[57]</a></sup> combined both with gliding and with flapping flight in <a href="/wiki/V_formation" title="V formation">V formation</a>, to commute distances of up to 150 km (93 mi) to feeding areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup> Pelicans also fly low (or "skim") over stretches of water, using a phenomenon known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_effect_(aircraft)" title="Ground effect (aircraft)">ground effect</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Lift-induced_drag" title="Lift-induced drag">drag</a> and increase <a href="/wiki/Lift_(force)" title="Lift (force)">lift</a>. As the air flows between the wings and the water surface it is compressed to a higher density and exerts a stronger upward force against the bird above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Hence substantial energy is saved while flying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adult pelicans rely on visual displays and behaviour to communicate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khanna_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-khanna-60">[60]</a></sup> particularly using their wings and bills. <a href="/wiki/Agonistic_behaviour" title="Agonistic behaviour">Agonistic behaviour</a> consists of thrusting and snapping at opponents with their bills, or lifting and waving their wings in a threatening manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Adult pelicans grunt when at the colony, but are generally silent elsewhere or outside breeding season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beaman_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-beaman-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dunne06_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-dunne06-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sasol_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-sasol-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Conversely, colonies are noisy as chicks vocalise extensively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khanna_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-khanna-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The diet of pelicans usually consists of fish, which can be up to 30 cm (1 ft) long,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-enc-50">[50]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, turtles, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and occasionally birds are also eaten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc1_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-66">[66]</a></sup> Aquatic prey is most commonly taken at or near the water surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mb_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-mb-49">[49]</a></sup> In deep water, white pelicans often fish alone. Nearer the shore, several will encircle schools of small fish or form a line to drive them into the shallows, beating their wings on the water surface and then scooping up the prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NationalGeographic_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-NationalGeographic-67">[67]</a></sup> They catch multiple small fish by expanding the throat pouch, which must be drained above the water surface before swallowing. This operation takes up to a minute, during which time other seabirds may <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">steal</a> the fish.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pelecanus_occidentalis_-Jamaica_-fishing-8.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Brown_pelican" title="Brown pelican">Brown pelicans</a> diving into the sea to catch fish in <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Large fish are caught with the bill-tip, then tossed up in the air to be caught and slid into the gullet head-first. A gull will sometimes stand on the pelican's head, peck it to distraction, and grab a fish from the open bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowStuffWorks_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-HowStuffWorks-68">[68]</a></sup> Pelicans in their turn sometimes snatch <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">prey</a> from other waterbirds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The brown pelican usually plunge-dives for its prey, especially for <a href="/wiki/Anchovy" title="Anchovy">anchovies</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usepa1980_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-usepa1980-69">[69]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Menhaden" title="Menhaden">menhaden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NationalGeographic_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-NationalGeographic-67">[67]</a></sup> Although principally a fish eater, the Australian pelican is also an eclectic and opportunistic <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavenger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivore</a> that forages in <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfill</a> sites as well as taking <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1b_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1b-70">[70]</a></sup> and "anything from insects and small crustaceans to ducks and small dogs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1b_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1b-70">[70]</a></sup> Food is not stored in a pelican's throat pouch, contrary to popular folklore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-enc-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Consumption of other birds by pelicans is rare, although great white pelicans have been observed swallowing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigeon" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._James%27s_Park" title="St. James's Park">St. James's Park</a> in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc2_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-66">[66]</a></sup> Spokeswoman for the Royal Parks Louise Wood opined that feeding on other birds is more likely with captive pelicans that live in a semi-urban environment and are in constant close contact with humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc2_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-66">[66]</a></sup> However, in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Cape" title="Western Cape">Western Cape</a> region of South Africa, biologist Marta de Ponte recorded the same species eating <a href="/wiki/Cape_gannet" title="Cape gannet">Cape gannet</a> chicks on <a href="/wiki/Malgas_Island" title="Malgas Island">Malgas Island</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Cape_cormorant" title="Cape cormorant">Cape cormorants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crowned_cormorant" title="Crowned cormorant">crowned cormorants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kelp_gull" title="Kelp gull">kelp gulls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greater_crested_tern" title="Greater crested tern">greater crested terns</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_penguin" title="African penguin">African penguins</a> on <a href="/wiki/Dassen_Island" title="Dassen Island">Dassen Island</a> and elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esr_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-esr-72">[72]</a></sup> Brown pelicans have been reported preying on young <a href="/wiki/Common_murre" title="Common murre">common murres</a> in California as well as the eggs and nestlings of <a href="/wiki/Cattle_egret" title="Cattle egret">cattle egrets</a> and nestling <a href="/wiki/Great_egret" title="Great egret">great egrets</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baja_California" title="Baja California">Baja California</a>, Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_and_lifespan">Breeding and lifespan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Breeding and lifespan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spot-billed_Pelican_(Pelecanus_philippensis)_at_nest_with_chicks_in_Uppalpadu_W_IMG_2663.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="1000" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_at_nest_with_chicks_in_Uppalpadu_W_IMG_2663.jpg/250px-Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_at_nest_with_chicks_in_Uppalpadu_W_IMG_2663.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_at_nest_with_chicks_in_Uppalpadu_W_IMG_2663.jpg/375px-Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_at_nest_with_chicks_in_Uppalpadu_W_IMG_2663.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_at_nest_with_chicks_in_Uppalpadu_W_IMG_2663.jpg/500px-Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_at_nest_with_chicks_in_Uppalpadu_W_IMG_2663.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
