</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spot-billed_Pelican_(Pelecanus_philippensis)_feeding_a_juvenile_in_Garapadu,_AP_W_IMG_5362.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="810" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_feeding_a_juvenile_in_Garapadu%2C_AP_W_IMG_5362.jpg/250px-Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_feeding_a_juvenile_in_Garapadu%2C_AP_W_IMG_5362.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_feeding_a_juvenile_in_Garapadu%2C_AP_W_IMG_5362.jpg/375px-Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_feeding_a_juvenile_in_Garapadu%2C_AP_W_IMG_5362.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_feeding_a_juvenile_in_Garapadu%2C_AP_W_IMG_5362.jpg/500px-Spot-billed_Pelican_%28Pelecanus_philippensis%29_feeding_a_juvenile_in_Garapadu%2C_AP_W_IMG_5362.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A spot-billed pelican feeding a juvenile in a nest in a tree at <a href="/wiki/Garapadu" title="Garapadu">Garapadu</a>, India</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelecanus_conspicillatus_-Brisbane_Water,_Broken_Bay,_New_South_Wales,_Australia_-colony-8.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1504" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Pelecanus_conspicillatus_-Brisbane_Water%2C_Broken_Bay%2C_New_South_Wales%2C_Australia_-colony-8.jpg/250px-Pelecanus_conspicillatus_-Brisbane_Water%2C_Broken_Bay%2C_New_South_Wales%2C_Australia_-colony-8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Pelecanus_conspicillatus_-Brisbane_Water%2C_Broken_Bay%2C_New_South_Wales%2C_Australia_-colony-8.jpg/375px-Pelecanus_conspicillatus_-Brisbane_Water%2C_Broken_Bay%2C_New_South_Wales%2C_Australia_-colony-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Pelecanus_conspicillatus_-Brisbane_Water%2C_Broken_Bay%2C_New_South_Wales%2C_Australia_-colony-8.jpg/500px-Pelecanus_conspicillatus_-Brisbane_Water%2C_Broken_Bay%2C_New_South_Wales%2C_Australia_-colony-8.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A nesting colony of <a href="/wiki/Australian_pelican" title="Australian pelican">Australian pelicans</a> on the coast of <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>, Australia. This species nests on the ground.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pelicans are <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gregarious" title="wikt:gregarious">gregarious</a> and nest colonially. Pairs are monogamous for a single season, but the pair bond extends only to the nesting area; mates are independent away from the nest. The ground-nesting (white) species have a complex communal courtship involving a group of males chasing a single female in the air, on land, or in the water while pointing, gaping, and thrusting their bills at each other. They can finish the process in a day. The tree-nesting species have a simpler process in which perched males advertise for females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-6"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup> The location of the breeding colony is constrained by the availability of an ample supply of fish to eat, although pelicans can use thermals to soar and commute for hundreds of kilometres daily to fetch food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-enc-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Australian pelican has two reproductive strategies depending on the local degree of environmental predictability. Colonies of tens or hundreds, rarely thousands, of birds breed regularly on small coastal and subcoastal islands where food is seasonally or permanently available. In arid inland Australia, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">endorheic</a> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Eyre_basin" title="Lake Eyre basin">Lake Eyre basin</a>, pelicans will breed opportunistically in very large numbers of up to 50,000 pairs, when irregular major floods, which may be many years apart, fill <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephemeral" title="Ephemeral">ephemeral</a> <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lakes</a> and provide large amounts of food for several months before drying out again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-ag-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>In all species copulation takes place at the nest site; it begins shortly after pairing and continues for 3–10 days before egg-laying. The male brings the nesting material, in ground-nesting species (which may not build a nest) sometimes in the pouch, and in tree-nesting species crosswise in the bill. The female then heaps the material up to form a simple structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-7"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The eggs are oval, white and coarsely textured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-7"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> All species normally lay at least two eggs; the usual clutch size is one to three, rarely up to six.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-8"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> Both sexes incubate with the eggs on top of or below the feet; they may display when changing shifts. Incubation takes 30–36 days;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-9"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> hatching success for undisturbed pairs can be as high as 95 percent but, because of sibling competition or <a href="/wiki/Siblicide" title="Siblicide">siblicide</a>, in the wild usually all but one nestling dies within the first few weeks (later in the pink-backed and spot-billed species). Both parents feed their young. Small chicks are fed by <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitation</a>; after about a week they are able to put their heads into their parent’s pouch and feed themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DofB_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-DofB-52">[52]</a></sup> Sometimes before, or especially after, being fed, they may seem to have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seizure" title="Seizure">seizure</a> that ends in falling unconscious; the reason is not clearly known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-8"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Parents of ground-nesting species sometimes drag older young around roughly by the head before feeding them. From about 25 days old,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-10"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> the young of these species gather in "pods" or "<a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A8che_(zoology)" title="Crèche (zoology)">crèches</a>" of up to 100 birds in which parents recognise and feed only their own offspring. By 6–8 weeks they wander around, occasionally swimming, and may practise communal feeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-9"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup> Young of all species <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a> 10–12 weeks after hatching. They may remain with their parents afterwards, but are now seldom or never fed. They are mature at three or four years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanzab1a_20-11"><a href="#cite_note-hanzab1a-20">[20]</a></sup> Overall breeding success is highly variable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-10"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup> Pelicans live for 15 to 25 years in the wild, although one reached an age of 54 years in captivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-enc-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_and_conservation">Status and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Status and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Populations">Populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Globally, pelican populations are adversely affected by four main factors: declining supplies of fish through overfishing or water pollution, destruction of habitat, direct effects of human activity such as disturbance at nesting colonies, hunting and culling, entanglement in fishing lines and hooks, and lastly the presence of pollutants such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> and <a href="/wiki/Endrin" title="Endrin">endrin</a>. Most species' populations are more or less stable, although three are classified by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> as being at risk. All species breed readily in zoos, which is potentially useful for conservation management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:(Pelecanus_occidentalis)_Tortuga_Bay_on_the_Island_of_Santa_Cruz,_Gal%C3%A1pagos.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/%28Pelecanus_occidentalis%29_Tortuga_Bay_on_the_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Gal%C3%A1pagos.JPG/220px-%28Pelecanus_occidentalis%29_Tortuga_Bay_on_the_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Gal%C3%A1pagos.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/%28Pelecanus_occidentalis%29_Tortuga_Bay_on_the_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Gal%C3%A1pagos.JPG/330px-%28Pelecanus_occidentalis%29_Tortuga_Bay_on_the_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Gal%C3%A1pagos.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/%28Pelecanus_occidentalis%29_Tortuga_Bay_on_the_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Gal%C3%A1pagos.JPG/440px-%28Pelecanus_occidentalis%29_Tortuga_Bay_on_the_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Gal%C3%A1pagos.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:(Pelecanus_occidentalis)_Tortuga_Bay_on_the_Island_of_Santa_Cruz,_Gal%C3%A1pagos.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelecanus_occidentalis" title="Pelecanus occidentalis">Pelecanus occidentalis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tortuga_Bay" title="Tortuga Bay">Tortuga Bay</a>, Island of Santa Cruz, Galápagos</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The combined population of brown and Peruvian pelicans is estimated at 650,000 birds, with around 250,000 in the United States and Caribbean, and 400,000 in Peru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[a]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Audubon_Society" title="National Audubon Society">National Audubon Society</a> estimates the global population of the brown pelican at 300,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Numbers of brown pelican plummeted in the 1950s and 1960s, largely as a consequence of environmental DDT pollution, and the species was listed as endangered in the US in 1970. With restrictions on DDT use in the US from 1972, its population has recovered, and it was delisted in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FWS_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FWS-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SF_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-SF-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Peruvian pelican is listed as Near Threatened because, although the population is estimated by <a href="/wiki/BirdLife_International" title="BirdLife International">BirdLife International</a> to exceed 500,000 mature individuals, and is possibly increasing, it has been much higher in the past. It declined dramatically during the 1998 <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o%E2%80%93Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño–Southern Oscillation">El Niño</a> event and could experience similar declines in the future. Conservation needs include regular monitoring throughout the range to determine population trends, particularly after El Niño years; restricting human access to important breeding colonies; and assessing interactions with fisheries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The spot-billed pelican has an estimated population between 13,000 and 18,000 and is considered to be Near Threatened in the IUCN Red List of Threatened Species. Numbers declined substantially during the 20th century, one crucial factor being the eradication of the important <a href="/wiki/Sittaung_River" title="Sittaung River">Sittaung valley</a> breeding colony in Burma through deforestation and the loss of feeding sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> The chief threats it faces are from habitat loss and human disturbance but populations have mostly stabilised following increased protection in India and Cambodia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucnphillippenis_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-iucnphillippenis-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The pink-backed pelican has a large population ranging over much of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>. In the absence of substantial threats or evidence of declines across its range, its conservation status is assessed as being of Least Concern. Regional threats include the drainage of wetlands and increasing disturbance in southern Africa. The species is susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulation</a> of toxins and to the destruction of nesting trees by logging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The American white pelican has increased in numbers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith05_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keith05-51">[51]</a></sup> with its population estimated at over 157,000 birds in 2005, becoming more numerous east of the continental divide while declining in the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> However it is unclear whether its numbers have been affected by exposure to pesticides as it has also lost habitat through wetland drainage and competition with recreational use of lakes and rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith05_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keith05-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelecanus_onocrotalus_-Kenya_-several-8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Pelecanus_onocrotalus_-Kenya_-several-8.jpg/220px-Pelecanus_onocrotalus_-Kenya_-several-8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Pelecanus_onocrotalus_-Kenya_-several-8.jpg/330px-Pelecanus_onocrotalus_-Kenya_-several-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Pelecanus_onocrotalus_-Kenya_-several-8.jpg/440px-Pelecanus_onocrotalus_-Kenya_-several-8.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pelecanus_onocrotalus_-Kenya_-several-8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Great_white_pelican" title="Great white pelican">Great white pelicans</a> loafing in Kenya</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Great white pelicans range over a large area of Africa and southern Asia. The overall trend in numbers is uncertain, with a mix of regional populations that are increasing, declining, stable or unknown, but there is no evidence of rapid overall decline and the status of the species is assessed as being of Least Concern. Threats include the drainage of wetlands, persecution and sport hunting, disturbance at the breeding colonies, and contamination by pesticides and heavy metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Dalmatian pelican is the rarest species with a population estimated at between 10,000 and 20,000 following massive declines in the 19th and 20th centuries. The main ongoing threats include hunting, especially in eastern Asia, disturbance, coastal development, collision with overhead power lines and the over-exploitation of fish stocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> It is listed as Vulnerable by the IUCN Red List of Threatened Species as the population trend is downwards, especially in Mongolia where it is nearly extinct. However, several colonies are increasing in size and the colony at the <a href="/wiki/Small_Prespa_Lake" title="Small Prespa Lake">Small Prespa Lake</a> in Greece has nearly 1000 breeding pairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucncrispus_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-iucncrispus-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Widespread across Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith05_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Keith05-51">[51]</a></sup> the Australian pelican has a population generally estimated at between 300,000 and 500,000 individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Overall population numbers fluctuate widely and erratically depending on wetland conditions and breeding success across the continent. The species is assessed as being of Least Concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culling_and_disturbance">Culling and disturbance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Culling and disturbance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pelicans have been persecuted by humans for their perceived competition for fish, despite the fact that their diet overlaps little with fish caught by people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith05_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-Keith05-51">[51]</a></sup> Starting in the 1880s, American white pelicans were clubbed and shot, their eggs and young were deliberately destroyed, and their feeding and nesting sites were degraded by water management schemes and wetland drainage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith05_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-Keith05-51">[51]</a></sup> Even in the 21st century, an increase in the population of American white pelicans in south-eastern Idaho in the US was seen to threaten the recreational <a href="/wiki/Cutthroat_trout" title="Cutthroat trout">cutthroat trout</a> fishery there, leading to official attempts to reduce pelican numbers through systematic harassment and <a href="/wiki/Culling" title="Culling">culling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Great white pelicans on Dyer Island, in the Western Cape region of South Africa, were culled during the 19th century because their predation of the eggs and chicks of <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a>-producing seabirds was seen to threaten the livelihood of the guano collectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esr_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-esr-72">[72]</a></sup> More recently, such predation at South African seabird colonies has impacted on the conservation of threatened seabird populations, especially crowned cormorants, Cape cormorants and <a href="/wiki/Bank_cormorant" title="Bank cormorant">bank cormorants</a>. This has led to suggestions that pelican numbers should be controlled at vulnerable colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esr_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-esr-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apart from habitat destruction and deliberate, targeted persecution, pelicans are vulnerable to disturbance at their breeding colonies by birdwatchers, photographers and other curious visitors. Human presence alone can cause the birds to accidentally displace or destroy their eggs, leave hatchlings exposed to predators and adverse weather, or even abandon their colonies completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfgc07_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-cfgc07-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poisoning_and_pollution">Poisoning and pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Poisoning and pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gulf-Oiled-Pelicans-June-3-2010.jpg"><img alt="Group of pelicans in captivity covered with oil" data-file-height="538" data-file-width="800" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Gulf-Oiled-Pelicans-June-3-2010.jpg/200px-Gulf-Oiled-Pelicans-June-3-2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Gulf-Oiled-Pelicans-June-3-2010.jpg/300px-Gulf-Oiled-Pelicans-June-3-2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Gulf-Oiled-Pelicans-June-3-2010.jpg/400px-Gulf-Oiled-Pelicans-June-3-2010.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Brown_pelican" title="Brown pelican">Brown pelicans</a>, covered with oil, after the <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a> of 2010</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelican_wash_from_oil_spill_Louisiana_13_Dawn_IBRRC_2010.05.04_B6X2141.jpg"><img alt="People washing oiled brown pelican" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="720" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Pelican_wash_from_oil_spill_Louisiana_13_Dawn_IBRRC_2010.05.04_B6X2141.jpg/200px-Pelican_wash_from_oil_spill_Louisiana_13_Dawn_IBRRC_2010.05.04_B6X2141.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Pelican_wash_from_oil_spill_Louisiana_13_Dawn_IBRRC_2010.05.04_B6X2141.jpg/300px-Pelican_wash_from_oil_spill_Louisiana_13_Dawn_IBRRC_2010.05.04_B6X2141.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Pelican_wash_from_oil_spill_Louisiana_13_Dawn_IBRRC_2010.05.04_B6X2141.jpg/400px-Pelican_wash_from_oil_spill_Louisiana_13_Dawn_IBRRC_2010.05.04_B6X2141.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Oiled brown pelican being washed at a rescue center in Fort Jackson, 2010</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>DDT pollution in the environment was a major cause of decline of brown pelican populations in North America in the 1950s and 1960s. It entered the oceanic <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food web</a>, contaminating and accumulating in several species, including one of the pelican’s primary food fish – the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_anchovy" title="Northern anchovy">northern anchovy</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Metabolite" title="Metabolite">metabolite</a> <a href="/wiki/Dichlorodiphenyldichloroethylene" title="Dichlorodiphenyldichloroethylene">DDE</a> is a reproductive <a href="/wiki/Toxicant" title="Toxicant">toxicant</a> in pelicans and many other birds, causing eggshell thinning and weakening, and consequent breeding failure through the eggs being accidentally crushed by brooding birds. Since an effective ban on the use of DDT was implemented in the US in 1972, the eggshells of breeding brown pelicans there have thickened and their populations have largely recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usepa1980_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-usepa1980-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1960s, following the major decline in brown pelican numbers in Louisiana from DDT poisoning, 500 pelicans were imported from Florida to augment and re-establish the population; over 300 subsequently died in April and May 1975 from poisoning by the pesticide endrin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> About 14,000 pelicans, including 7500 American white pelicans, perished from <a href="/wiki/Botulism" title="Botulism">botulism</a> after eating fish from the <a href="/wiki/Salton_Sea" title="Salton Sea">Salton Sea</a> in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keith05_51-6"><a href="#cite_note-Keith05-51">[51]</a></sup> In 1991 abnormal numbers of brown pelicans and <a href="/wiki/Brandt%27s_cormorant" title="Brandt's cormorant">Brandt's cormorants</a> died at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Cruz,_California" title="Santa Cruz, California">Santa Cruz, California</a>, when their food fish (anchovies) were contaminated with <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxin" title="Neurotoxin">neurotoxic</a> <a href="/wiki/Domoic_acid" title="Domoic acid">domoic acid</a>, produced by the <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">diatom</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudo-nitzschia" title="Pseudo-nitzschia">Pseudo-nitzschia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>As waterbirds that feed on fish, pelicans are highly susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a>, both directly by being oiled and by the impact on their food resources. A 2007 report to the California Fish and Game Commission estimated that, during the previous 20 years, some 500–1000 brown pelicans had been affected by oil spills in California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfgc07_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-cfgc07-90">[89]</a></sup> A 2011 report by the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Biological_Diversity" title="Center for Biological Diversity">Center for Biological Diversity</a>, a year after the April 2010 <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a>, said that 932 brown pelicans had been collected after being affected by oiling and estimated that ten times that number had been harmed as a result of the spill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Where pelicans interact with fishers, through either sharing the same waters or scavenging for fishing refuse, they are especially vulnerable to being hooked and entangled in both active and discarded fishing lines. <a href="/wiki/Fish_hook" title="Fish hook">Fish hooks</a> are swallowed or catch in the skin of the pouch or webbed feet, and strong <a href="/wiki/Monofilament_fishing_line" title="Monofilament fishing line">monofilament fishing line</a> can become wound around bill, wings or legs, resulting in crippling, starvation, and often death. Local rescue organisations have been established in North America and Australia by volunteers to treat and rehabilitate injured pelicans and other wildlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites_and_disease">Parasites and disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Parasites and disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As with other bird families, pelicans are susceptible to a variety of <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a>. Specialist <a href="/wiki/Bird_louse" title="Bird louse">feather lice</a> of the genus <i>Piagetella</i> are found in the pouches of all species of pelican, but are otherwise only known from New World and Antarctic cormorants. <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">Avian malaria</a> is carried by the <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquito</a> <i>Culex pipens</i>, and high densities of these biting insects may force pelican colonies to be abandoned. <a href="/wiki/Leech" title="Leech">Leeches</a> may attach to the <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">vent</a> or sometimes the inside of the pouch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothschild_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-98">[97]</a></sup> A study of the parasites of the American white pelican found 75 different species, including <a href="/wiki/Cestoda" title="Cestoda">tapeworms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trematoda" title="Trematoda">flukes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">flies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">fleas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>. Many of these do little harm, but flies may be implicated in the death of nestlings, particularly if they are weak or unwell, and the <a href="/wiki/Argasidae" title="Argasidae">soft tick</a> <i>Ornithodoros capensis</i> sometimes causes adults to desert the nest. Many pelican parasites are found in other bird groups, but several <a href="/wiki/Louse" title="Louse">lice</a> are very <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host-specific</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overstreet_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-overstreet-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Healthy pelicans can usually cope with their lice, but sick birds may carry hundreds of individuals, which hastens their demise. The pouch louse <i>Piagetiella peralis</i>, which occurs in the pouch and therefore cannot be removed by preening, is usually not a serious problem, even when present in such numbers that it covers the whole interior of the pouch, but sometimes inflammation and bleeding may harm the host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overstreet_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-overstreet-99">[98]</a></sup> The brown pelican has a similarly extensive range of parasites. The nematodes <i>Contracaecum multipapillatum</i> and <i>C. mexicanum</i> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trematode" title="Trematode">trematode</a> <i>Ribeiroia ondatrae</i> have caused illness and mortality in the Puerto Rican population, possibly endangering the pelican on this island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dyer_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-dyer-100">[99]</a></sup> In May 2012, hundreds of Peruvian pelicans were reported to have perished in Peru from a combination of starvation and roundworm infestation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peru_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-peru-101">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion.2C_mythology.2C_and_popular_culture">Religion, mythology, and popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Religion, mythology, and popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%84gyptische_Sammlung_08.jpg"><img alt=" Egyptian temple relief detail of pelicans" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="480" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/%C3%84gyptische_Sammlung_08.jpg/170px-%C3%84gyptische_Sammlung_08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/%C3%84gyptische_Sammlung_08.jpg/255px-%C3%84gyptische_Sammlung_08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/%C3%84gyptische_Sammlung_08.jpg/340px-%C3%84gyptische_Sammlung_08.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%84gyptische_Sammlung_08.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pelicans on a <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Fifth Dynasty of Egypt">Fifth Dynasty</a> <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Abu_Gorab" title="Abu Gorab">Abu Gorab</a> temple, Egypt</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The pelican (<i>Henet</i> in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a>) was associated in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> with death and the <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>. It was depicted in art on the walls of tombs, and figured in <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funerary</a> texts, as a protective symbol against snakes. Henet was also referred to in the <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_Texts" title="Pyramid Texts">Pyramid Texts</a> as the "mother of the king" and thus seen as a goddess. References in non-royal funerary <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyri</a> show that the pelican was believed to possess the ability to prophesy safe passage in the <a href="/wiki/Underworld" title="Underworld">underworld</a> for someone who had died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Consumption of pelican, as with other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seabirds" title="Seabirds">seabirds</a>, is considered non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kosher" title="Kosher">kosher</a> as an '<a href="/wiki/Unclean_animal#Judaism" title="Unclean animal">unclean animal</a>', and thus forbidden in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_dietary_law" title="Jewish dietary law">Jewish dietary law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Origin_myth" title="Origin myth">origin myth</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Murri_people" title="Murri people">Murri people</a> of <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>, cited by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lang" title="Andrew Lang">Andrew Lang</a>, describes how the Australian pelican acquired its black and white plumage. The pelican, formerly a black bird, made a canoe during a flood in order to save drowning people. He fell in love with a woman he thus saved, but she and her friends tricked him and escaped. The pelican consequently prepared to go to war against them by daubing himself with white clay as war paint. However, before he had finished, another pelican, on seeing such a strange piebald creature, killed him with its beak, and all such pelicans have been black and white ever since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Moche_culture" title="Moche culture">Moche</a> people of ancient <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> worshipped nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> They placed emphasis on animals and often depicted pelicans in their art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alcatraz_Island" title="Alcatraz Island">Alcatraz Island</a> was given its name by the Spanish because of the number of large numbers of brown pelicans nesting present. The word <i>alcatraz</i> is itself derived from the Arabic "al-caduos", a term used for a water-carrying vessel and likened to the pouch of the pelican. The English name albatross is also derived by corruption of the Spanish word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bergatreute_Pfarrkirche_Hochaltar_Vogelnest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="646" data-file-width="646" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bergatreute_Pfarrkirche_Hochaltar_Vogelnest.jpg/220px-Bergatreute_Pfarrkirche_Hochaltar_Vogelnest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bergatreute_Pfarrkirche_Hochaltar_Vogelnest.jpg/330px-Bergatreute_Pfarrkirche_Hochaltar_Vogelnest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bergatreute_Pfarrkirche_Hochaltar_Vogelnest.jpg/440px-Bergatreute_Pfarrkirche_Hochaltar_Vogelnest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bergatreute_Pfarrkirche_Hochaltar_Vogelnest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of pelican wounding its breast to feed its chicks</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Poster_The_Scottish_National_Blood_Transfusion_Association.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3222" data-file-width="2513" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Poster_The_Scottish_National_Blood_Transfusion_Association.JPG/220px-Poster_The_Scottish_National_Blood_Transfusion_Association.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Poster_The_Scottish_National_Blood_Transfusion_Association.JPG/330px-Poster_The_Scottish_National_Blood_Transfusion_Association.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Poster_The_Scottish_National_Blood_Transfusion_Association.JPG/440px-Poster_The_Scottish_National_Blood_Transfusion_Association.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poster_The_Scottish_National_Blood_Transfusion_Association.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
WWII 1944 Scottish Blood Transfusion Poster</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> Europe, the pelican was thought to be particularly attentive to her young, to the point of providing her own blood by wounding her own breast when no other food was available. As a result, the pelican came to symbolise the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passion_(Christianity)" title="Passion (Christianity)">Passion</a> of Jesus and the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> and usurped the image of the <a href="/wiki/Lamb_of_God" title="Lamb of God">lamb and the flag</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgrath_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcgrath-111">[110]</a></sup> A reference to this mythical characteristic is contained for example in the hymn by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Saint Thomas Aquinas">Saint Thomas Aquinas</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Adoro_te_devote" title="Adoro te devote">Adoro te devote</a>" or "Humbly We Adore Thee", where in the penultimate verse he describes Christ as the "loving divine pelican, able to provide nourishment from his breast".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I of England</a> adopted the symbol, portraying herself as the "mother of the Church of England". <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hilliard" title="Nicholas Hilliard">Nicholas Hilliard</a> painted the <i>Pelican Portrait</i> in around 1573, now owned by the <a href="/wiki/Walker_Art_Gallery" title="Walker Art Gallery">Walker Art Gallery</a> in Liverpool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> A pelican feeding her young is depicted in an oval panel at the bottom of the title page of the first (1611) edition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" title="Authorized King James Version">King James Bible</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgrath_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-mcgrath-111">[110]</a></sup> Such "a pelican in her piety" appears in the 1686 <a href="/wiki/Reredos" title="Reredos">reredos</a> by <a href="/wiki/Grinling_Gibbons" title="Grinling Gibbons">Grinling Gibbons</a> in the church of <a href="/wiki/St_Mary_Abchurch" title="St Mary Abchurch">St Mary Abchurch</a> in the City of London. Earlier medieval examples of the motif appear in painted murals, for example that of c. 1350 in the <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish church</a> of <a href="/wiki/Belchamp_Walter" title="Belchamp Walter">Belchamp Walter</a>, Essex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nicholas_Hilliard_(called)_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4427" data-file-width="3407" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nicholas_Hilliard_(called)_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Queen Elizabeth I: the <i>Pelican Portrait</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hilliard" title="Nicholas Hilliard">Nicholas Hilliard</a> (c. 1573), in which <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a> wears the medieval symbol of the pelican on her chest</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The self-sacrificial aspect of the pelican was reinforced by the widely read medieval <a href="/wiki/Bestiary" title="Bestiary">bestiaries</a>. The device of "a pelican in her piety" or "a pelican vulning (from Latin <i>vulno</i>, "to wound") herself" was used in <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a>. An older version of the myth is that the pelican used to kill its young then resurrect them with its blood, again analogous to the sacrifice of Jesus. Likewise, a folktale from <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> says that a pelican killed her young by rough treatment but was then so contrite that she resurrected them with her own blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-11"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The legends of self-wounding and the provision of blood may have arisen because of the impression a pelican sometimes gives that it is stabbing itself with its bill. In reality, it often presses this onto its chest in order to fully empty the pouch. Another possible derivation is the tendency of the bird to rest with its bill on its breast; the Dalmatian pelican has a blood-red pouch in the early breeding season and this may have contributed to the myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSS_19-12"><a href="#cite_note-NSS-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heraldry">Heraldry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Heraldry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Pelicans have featured extensively in heraldry, generally using the Christian symbolism of the pelican as a caring and self-sacrificing parent. The image became linked to the medieval religious feast of <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Christi_(feast)" title="Corpus Christi (feast)">Corpus Christi</a>. The universities of Oxford and Cambridge each have colleges named for the religious festival nearest the dates of their establishment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgrath_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-mcgrath-111">[110]</a></sup> and both <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Christi_College,_Cambridge" title="Corpus Christi College, Cambridge">Corpus Christi College, Cambridge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corpus_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corpus-115">[114]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Christi_College,_Oxford" title="Corpus Christi College, Oxford">Corpus Christi College, Oxford</a>, feature pelicans on their coats of arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>The medical faculties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_University_in_Prague" title="Charles University in Prague">Charles University in Prague</a> also have a pelican as their emblem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> The symbol of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Blood_Transfusion_Service" title="Irish Blood Transfusion Service">Irish Blood Transfusion Service</a> is a pelican, and for most of its existence the headquarters of the service was located at Pelican House in Dublin, Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> The heraldic pelican also ended up as a pub name and image, though sometimes with the image of the ship <i><a href="/wiki/Golden_Hind" title="Golden Hind">Golden Hind</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a>'s famous ship was initially called <i>Pelican</i>, and adorned the British <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British pre-decimal coin)">halfpenny coin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_usage">Modern usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albanien2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="381" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Albanien2.jpg/220px-Albanien2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Albanien2.jpg/330px-Albanien2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/37/Albanien2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albanien2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pelican on the Albanian 1 <a href="/wiki/Albanian_lek" title="Albanian lek">lek</a> coin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The great white pelican is the national bird of <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> The brown pelican is the national bird of three Caribbean countries—<a href="/wiki/Saint_Kitts_and_Nevis" title="Saint Kitts and Nevis">Saint Kitts and Nevis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sint_Maarten" title="Sint Maarten">Sint Maarten</a>—and features on their <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coats of arms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> It is also the state bird of the US state of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, which is known colloquially as the Pelican State; the bird appears on the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Louisiana" title="Flag of Louisiana">state flag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seal_of_Louisiana" title="Seal of Louisiana">state seal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-4">[4]</a></sup> It adorns the seals of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_State_University" title="Louisiana State University">Louisiana State University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tulane_University" title="Tulane University">Tulane University</a>, and is the mascot of the <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_Pelicans" title="New Orleans Pelicans">New Orleans Pelicans</a> NBA team, Tulane University, and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_West_Indies" title="University of the West Indies">University of the West Indies</a>. A white pelican logo is used by the Portuguese bank <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montepio_Geral" title="Montepio Geral">Montepio Geral</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-montepio_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-montepio-125">[124]</a></sup> and a pelican is depicted on the <a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">reverse</a> of the Albanian 1 <a href="/wiki/Albanian_lek" title="Albanian lek">lek</a> coin, issued in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coin_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-coin-126">[125]</a></sup> The name and image were used for <a href="/wiki/Penguin_Books#Pelican_books" title="Penguin Books">Pelican Books</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Imprint_(trade_name)" title="Imprint (trade name)">imprint</a> of non-fiction books published by <a href="/wiki/Penguin_Books" title="Penguin Books">Penguin Books</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-OED-4">[4]</a></sup> The seal of the <a href="/wiki/Packer_Collegiate_Institute" title="Packer Collegiate Institute">Packer Collegiate Institute</a>, a pelican feeding her young, has been in use since 1885.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Christian Democratic political party known as the <a href="/wiki/American_Solidarity_Party" title="American Solidarity Party">American Solidarity Party</a> uses the pelican as its animal symbol, alluding to its Catholic social teaching platform.</p>
<p>The pelican is the subject of a popular <a href="/wiki/Limerick_(poetry)" title="Limerick (poetry)">limerick</a> originally composed by <a href="/wiki/Dixon_Lanier_Merritt" title="Dixon Lanier Merritt">Dixon Lanier Merritt</a> in 1910 with several variations by other authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> The original version ran:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="poem">
<p><span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">A wonderful bird is the pelican,</span><br/>
<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">His bill will hold more than his belican,</span><br/>
<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">He can take in his beak</span><br/>
<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">Food enough for a week,</span><br/>
<span class="mw-poem-indented" style="display: inline-block; margin-left: 1em;">But I'm damned if I see how the helican.</span></p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The US Government has not accepted the elevation of the two taxa into separate species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FWS_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FWS-75">[75]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelican&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
